    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ce661a84-f868-439e-a026-7cb91a89ee2c'

<span id="freeTextContainer13099878329286223116"><i>These are the secrets I have kept. This is the trust I never betrayed.   <span id="freeText13099878329286223116" style="display:none"><i>These are the secrets I have kept. This is the trust I never betrayed. But he is dead now and has been for more than forty years, the one who gave me his trust, the one for whom I kept these secrets. The one who saved me . . . and the one who cursed me.</i><br /><br />So starts the diary of Will Henry, orphaned assistant to Dr. Pellinore Warthorpe, a man with a most unusual specialty: monstrumology, the study of monsters. In his time with the doctor, Will has met many a mysterious late-night visitor, and seen things he never imagined were real. But when a grave robber comes calling in the middle of the night with a gruesome find, he brings with him their most deadly case yet.<br /><br />A gothic tour de force that explores the darkest heart of man and monster and asks the question: When does man become the very thing he hunts?<br /></span>

<div id="review_181859317" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181859317" />
    <a title="Maggie Stiefvater" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1246837-maggie-stiefvater"><img alt="Maggie Stiefvater" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587234846p2/1246837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181859317?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maggie Stiefvater" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maggie Stiefvater" href="/user/show/1246837-maggie-stiefvater">Maggie Stiefvater</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1246837-maggie-stiefvater?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1246837-maggie-stiefvater?shelf=recommended">recommended</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181859317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10325205979657860785">Soooo this one is about a rather particular Monstrumologist and his apprentice chasing headless man-eating monsters across Victorian New England. <br/><br/>Here are five reasons why you should read it:<br/>1. These are proper monsters. They don’t want to make out with you or play you songs on their guitar while you snuggle on the sofa. They just want to eat you, except for when they want to insert their babies in your corpse so they have something to snack on as they incubate. Okay, it’s a little gross sometim</span>
  <span id="freeText10325205979657860785" style="display:none">Soooo this one is about a rather particular Monstrumologist and his apprentice chasing headless man-eating monsters across Victorian New England. <br /><br />Here are five reasons why you should read it:<br />1. These are proper monsters. They don’t want to make out with you or play you songs on their guitar while you snuggle on the sofa. They just want to eat you, except for when they want to insert their babies in your corpse so they have something to snack on as they incubate. Okay, it’s a little gross sometimes. I ought to say that up front.<br /><br />2. The voice! The voice! Apart from the first and last chapters, which are introduced in modern times (and which I don’t care for), the entire novel is told from the point of view of Will Henry, the Monstrumologist’s pint sized apprentice. He is resolute but afraid, put upon but never whiny. I love the historical aspect. It’s all very gaslight and cobblestones and black cloaks and gasping behind hands.<br /><br />3. The Monstromologist! He is so high-maintenance and flawed and persnickety. Basically, he is Howl from Howl’s Moving Castle, if Howl never met Sophie. Oh, my love is undying. “WILL HENRY, SNAP TO!”<br /><br />4. I wish I could just make you read this book now.<br /><br />5. The beginning. Also, the middle. Also, the end. There is a character twist two thirds of the way through the book that I just did not see and I literally gasped on a plane. Then I was so delighted that a book had made me gasp on a plane that I punched Lover in the shoulder and made wild hand gestures. This book is put together like a puzzle box, and I will be taking it apart again sometime soon.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10325205979657860785" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1378505400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1378505400" />
    <a title="Aj the Ravenous Reader" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader"><img alt="Aj the Ravenous Reader" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557800982p2/30248594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1378505400?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aj the Ravenous Reader" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aj the Ravenous Reader" href="/user/show/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader">Aj the Ravenous Reader</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aj the Ravenous Reader by: </span>
      Vane, Tash, Paige and Evelyn 
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader?shelf=physically-owned-books">physically-owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30248594-aj-the-ravenous-reader?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1378505400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16944699265356068333"><br/>People who know me are very well aware that I wouldn't in a million years pick up a a horror-monster book of my own accord. But I have this personal reading challenge in which I am aiming to read at least one book recommended by each of my GR friends and I thought reading this would be like hitting four birds in one stone. But instead of birds, I would be hitting four intelligent, opinionated and not to mention, gorgeous young women's recommendation, for these four lovely ladies-<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003925660?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Vane</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1282901803?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tash</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1357347740?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Paig</a></span>
  <span id="freeText16944699265356068333" style="display:none"><br>People who know me are very well aware that I wouldn't in a million years pick up a a horror-monster book of my own accord. But I have this personal reading challenge in which I am aiming to read at least one book recommended by each of my GR friends and I thought reading this would be like hitting four birds in one stone. But instead of birds, I would be hitting four intelligent, opinionated and not to mention, gorgeous young women's recommendation, for these four lovely ladies-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003925660?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Vane</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1282901803?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Tash</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1357347740?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Paige</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262353135?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Evelyn</a> are head over heels for this novel (especially the first three^^). (Click their names for their review.^^)Plus, I am a huge fan of Uncle Rick's Fifth Wave series, so this is practically a win-win.<br><br>After reading the book, I perfectly understand why any reader who is fan of horror, mystery, pure gore, dry humor, mystery, perfectly embodied characters of the Victorian era, pure literary creativity and imagination and exemplary intelligent writing would fall crazily in love with this book. I did fall in love with the latter half of these attributes I mentioned, the first three though...<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1425821912ra/13938055.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I have to admit that the thing that blew me away in this novel is not so much the content. The story is mainly about the 12 year old Will Jenry James' account of his out of the ordinary experience as the loyal assistant of Dr. Pellinore Warthrop, a mad scientist, a "monstrumologist" obsessed with his craft that he had become <i>
  <b>"flummoxed by the precepts of normal conversations"</b>
</i> (this is the writing language throughout the novel^^) and their encounter with these human eating preys called, Anthropophagi which perfectly shaped children's imagination of a monster. Come to think of it, stories like this have been already told by hundreds of dads to their children as a simple bedtime story. Okay, maybe not as a bedtime story but you get what I mean.<br><br>But like in any piece of art, the meaning is essentially of no value if not for the form it is conveyed through. That is what gave this book the huge difference. It is perfectly executed with a strong narrative voice, true to life characters, powerful general message and thought provoking philosophical truths about humanity, intensely engaging plot and precise descriptive imagery. Uncle Rick Yancey has superhuman writing abilities. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1434346415ra/15207709.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>At times though, the gory descriptions were too vivid, specific and overly gut-wrenching. There were scenes that made me really nauseous. I was literally sick to my stomach especially that part with Capt. Varner. <b>Friendly advice:</b> Do not read this right after eating, before sleeping, when you are at a hospital, when you are not feeling well. <br><br>This is a certified literary masterpiece although not in this lifetime or another will I ever reread this. <br><br>If you are a fan of this genre, then this is written specifically for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16944699265356068333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_125023269" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125023269" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125023269?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=why-yes-i-ya">why-yes-i-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=and-so-this-is-grad-school">and-so-this-is-grad-school</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125023269" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9656965766876760271">okay, so monsters.<br/><br/>this reads like victorian teen fiction, only with more arterial spray. it's got all the trappings: it is long, and there are orphans and mad scientists, an evil madhouse director, and then there are monsters that eat people. <br/><br/>there is absolutely no crossover audience between this and <i>twilight</i>. the girls who swoon over edward's restrained bloodlust are going to be horrified by the multiple beheadings and the scene where a child is reduced to a fine mist of blood splatter-painting</span>
  <span id="freeText9656965766876760271" style="display:none">okay, so monsters.<br><br>this reads like victorian teen fiction, only with more arterial spray. it's got all the trappings: it is long, and there are orphans and mad scientists, an evil madhouse director, and then there are monsters that eat people. <br><br>there is absolutely no crossover audience between this and <i>twilight</i>. the girls who swoon over edward's restrained bloodlust are going to be horrified by the multiple beheadings and the scene where a child is reduced to a fine mist of blood splatter-painting a living room. <br><br>so why isn't this book awesome?<br><br>years ago, before i was captured and dragged kicking and screaming into the Land of Teen Fiction, i had a certain conception of what teen fiction was "like," and i thought "this is not for me, it is for teens and unbright adults." and then i read some really great teen fiction, and felt ashamed for being so dismissive without having done any actual legwork. <br><br>but this book is pretty much the way i thought teen fiction was before my conversion. it's fine...for kids. it's got a fast pace; even though it is long, you just burn right through it, there are a lot of action sequences, the characters aren't terribly original or developed, but it's all about the monsters, right? and the gore. <br><br>it's not awful, it's just not terribly sophisticated, despite its references to jonathan swift and shakespeare (william) and herodotus and other classical mythologies....oh, and the kid's name is william henry james. so, there's that...you know, for the kids.<br><br>but, it is probably a fine, quick halloween read. i shrugz.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9656965766876760271" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1388736587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1388736587" />
    <a title="Sean Gibson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20955173-sean-gibson"><img alt="Sean Gibson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416866667p2/20955173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1388736587?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Gibson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Gibson" href="/user/show/20955173-sean-gibson">Sean Gibson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1388736587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4916391629338393079">Before getting into any substantive thoughts on this book, I’d like to take a moment to note that if I made the study of monsters my life’s work, I’d come up with a much, much better name for my profession than “monstrumologist.” I mean, do proctologists call themselves “ass pokers?” Of course not—just because they have their hands up there all day doesn’t mean their heads are, too. See, cryptozoologists are smart—they figured out how to have a ridiculous field of study but make it sound fancy. </span>
  <span id="freeText4916391629338393079" style="display:none">Before getting into any substantive thoughts on this book, I’d like to take a moment to note that if I made the study of monsters my life’s work, I’d come up with a much, much better name for my profession than “monstrumologist.” I mean, do proctologists call themselves “ass pokers?” Of course not—just because they have their hands up there all day doesn’t mean their heads are, too. See, cryptozoologists are smart—they figured out how to have a ridiculous field of study but make it sound fancy. Take a lesson, Pellinore.<br /><br />Okay, now for the book itself—let's call it 3.5 stars.<br /><br />There are numerous reasons why I should have loved this book: <br /><br />-Creepy Victorian-era setting (albeit on the other side of the pond) and appropriately florid, descriptive writing style (you all know I’m a sucker for books that use 5 words in instances where 1 would do just fine)<br /><br />-Strange things afoot, including the appearance of terrifying, murderous creatures<br /><br />-Shadowy pasts and dark deeds to atone for<br /><br />-Character named for King Pellinore (respect: dude broke the sword that Arthur drew from the stone)<br /><br />-Scones. Because delicious<br /><br />So, why is Vane going to kill me for only giving this 3.5 stars?<br /><br />It came down to the characters. This is a solidly entertaining story when it comes to the particulars of the aforementioned murderous creatures, how they got to the U.S. of A., and how our merry band of heroes went about trying to put an end to their reign of horrifically graphic body desecrations (I’m not sure I’ve ever read anything more graphic than this book when it comes to descriptions of dismemberments, incidentally…how is this YA??). The characters, though…I just didn’t get attached to anyone. Our narrator, Will Henry is…well, he’s fine. Plucky young lad and all that. But, that’s it. He’s fine. He doesn’t really inspire strong feelings one way or the other. <br /><br />And then there’s the ass poker, er, monstrumologist, Dr. Pellinore Warthrop. Aside from the awesome first name, he’s basically an assholier version of Sherlock Holmes (and Holmes, though we love him, was, himself, a colossal, gaping asshole). <br /><br />Look, characters don’t need to be good and selfless and noble to elicit admiration or be interesting. Tyrion Lannister is the most compelling character in the Song of Ice and Fire, right? And he killed his father. With a crossbow. While he was defecating (his father, I mean…would have been quite a feat if Tyrion had offed Tywin with a crossbow while he himself was defecating). But, they DO need to be more than just a psychotically focused genius with zero people skills.<br /><br />Consider, if I can reference an amazing character from another medium, Dr. Gregory House. Like Warthrop, House is a derivation on Holmes (an even more obvious one, of course, given his synonymous surname). House is just as misanthropic as Warthrop, just as singularly focused, just as abusive to the people who try to love and care for him. But, House is still a sympathetic character – we may not like what he does, but we like HIM. Why? Pick a reason—his pain humanizes him, his reasoning is sound even if his methods are madness, he shows flashes of tenderness toward marginalized members of society, he’s freaking hilarious…all of these characteristics make House a compelling character (it doesn’t hurt that his Watson, Dr. James Wilson, provides the idealistic moral counterbalance to his realist pragmatism, something that Will Henry has neither the gravitas nor the boyish good looks to do (okay, well, he probably has the latter).<br /><br />Warthrop, however, lacks those redeeming qualities. Sure, he seems to care for Will Henry…sort of. Otherwise, he’s pretty much just a jerk, and for all of his purported intelligence, he’s right about as often as a stopped clock. Or a monkey with a typewriter. <br /><br />And that’s what kept this from being a really great book. It’s well written and has an interesting conceit. But, it’s still a solid read. I’m just not sure if I’m going to continue with the series…<br /><br />(Go ahead and proceed with slaying me anthropophagi-style, Vane.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4916391629338393079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1282901803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1282901803" />
    <a title="♛Tash" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5451856-tash"><img alt="♛Tash" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456286012p2/5451856.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1282901803?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="♛Tash" class="user" itemprop="url" name="♛Tash" href="/user/show/5451856-tash">♛Tash</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8161464-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to ♛Tash by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5451856-tash?shelf=from-authors-i-like">from-authors-i-like</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1282901803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13446698390304216326"><b>5 morally ambiguous stars and a fangirly, gify and, possibly, incoherent review.</b><br/><br/>Buddy read with my precious <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/15550986-paige-bookdragon' rel='nofollow noopener'>BookDragon</a>.<br/><br/><b>Q: What do you get when you cross Supernatural and Sherlock?</b><br/><b>A: Me<br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1411345876ra/11217541.gif' width='304' height='171' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/></b><br/><br/>Yes, I am a shameless fan girl for both shows. Not even sorry, but it gets awkward for onlookers when I fangirl in public. I’ve been asked more than I care to count what are in these shows for me to completely lose my shit over them?<br/><br/>What indeed? I love both shows because they deal with mysteries and the macabre, th</span>
  <span id="freeText13446698390304216326" style="display:none"><b>5 morally ambiguous stars and a fangirly, gify and, possibly, incoherent review.</b><br><br>Buddy read with my precious <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/15550986-paige-bookdragon" rel="nofollow noopener">BookDragon</a>.<br><br><b>Q: What do you get when you cross Supernatural and Sherlock?</b><br><b>A: Me<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1411345876ra/11217541.gif" width="304" height="171" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></b><br><br>Yes, I am a shameless fan girl for both shows. Not even sorry, but it gets awkward for onlookers when I fangirl in public. I’ve been asked more than I care to count what are in these shows for me to completely lose my shit over them?<br><br>What indeed? I love both shows because they deal with mysteries and the macabre, they’re infused with equal parts humor and tragedy, and lastly, they both have charming main and supporting characters. Not that I care, the main characters are,eh, somewhat pleasing to the eyes too.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439677709ra/15871258.gif" width="304" height="171" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And what the heck does it have to do with The Monstrumologist? I loved this book to gory bits for the same reasons as I love Superlock and let me breakdown the ways:<br><br><b>*Characters</b> – typical to Victorian gothic, we have the mad scientist, the orphan, the seemingly incompetent constable and, uh, a Van Helsing-y monster hunter. Our narrator is 12 year old orphan straight from a Dickens book named Will Henry. He tells us of his adventures as an assistant to New England’s premiere Monstrumologist (doctor of the study of monsters) , Pellinore Warthrop, who is…<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436682697ra/15492140.gif" width="304" height="122" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Then we have our extremely charming and pragmatic gun for hire/monster hunter, John Kearns, who has zero delusions of morality. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439673914i/15870729.gif" width="304" height="171" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>All the main characters in this book are morally ambiguous. Their actions invoke that age old conundrum of the double-effect reasoning. Needless to say, I loved the characters exactly for this complexity.<br><br><i>P.S. The main characters are all male. I guess it’s understandable given that science and law-keeping were exclusive for males in the Victorian era, but there better be ladies in the next few books.</i><br><br><b>*Setting</b> – set in a fictional NE town called New Jerusalem, we have all the landscapes typical of Victorian gothic. We have the misty graveyard complete with intricate mausoleums, townhouses with underground laboratories and tiny lofts for servants or orphaned assistants, and decrepit insane asylums. Admittedly, the setting isn’t that distinctive for this book, but it was done well enough for the story.<br><br><b>*Plot and pacing</b> – the best features of the book, imo. Despite the hefty 454 pages, this was a fast paced read. We get action from the start to the end. I’ve read The Fifth Wave books so I know that Rick Yancey doesn’t shy away from blood and gore, but he overdid himself here. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439673915ra/15870730.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>He does <i>ew</i> to full on <i>hoark</i>. I am not squeamish at all, but damn you Rick Yancey for that pus in the mouth scene.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439674081ra/15870745.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And the monsters, y’all, stuff of nightmares! Not even romanticized a bit, there are no vampires suffering from ennui or tortured geniuses in love with naïve ingénue here. We get monsters of the primeval sort, I’d like to say that they’re dumb but they’re not, they are intelligent in a way predators are, unhampered by conscience. We also have monsters of the human variety in this book.<br><br>To end my ramblings, here is a quote from this novel, which I think perfectly captures the essence of the book: <i>“We are slaves, all of us...Some are slaves to fear. Others are slaves to reason—or base desire. It is our lot to be slaves...and the question must be to what shall we owe our indenture? Will it be to truth or to falsehood, hope or despair, light or darkness? I choose to serve the light, even though that bondage often lies in darkness.” </i>“</span>
  <a data-text-id="13446698390304216326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1320929442" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1320929442" />
    <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner"><img alt="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1532688964p2/31461418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1320929442?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘" href="/user/show/31461418-alienor-french-frowner">Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Alienor ✘ French Frowner ✘ by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=in-a-minority-here">in-a-minority-here</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=yes-i-m-disappointed">yes-i-m-disappointed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31461418-alienor-french-frowner?shelf=mystery-thriller-horror">mystery-thriller-horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1320929442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer409990499423395726"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443292262i/16344475._SX540_.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>If this book was a landscape, it would be the Sahara desert : perhaps beautiful from a distance, but so fucking <i>dry</i> that I wouldn't want to stay there more than 1 hour. <b>I stopped at 67%, because there's just so much boredom I can take.</b><br/><br/>► <b>Lack of ... <i>interest</i></b>. What's the point of this? Oh, here's a monster. Look, his teeth are in his belly. This is a *insert Latin name to appear clever*. How wonderful. Now, you can eat your porridge, but just so you know, these creatures are invading New Jerusalem</span>
  <span id="freeText409990499423395726" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443292262i/16344475._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If this book was a landscape, it would be the Sahara desert : perhaps beautiful from a distance, but so fucking <i>dry</i> that I wouldn't want to stay there more than 1 hour. <b>I stopped at 67%, because there's just so much boredom I can take.</b><br><br>► <b>Lack of ... <i>interest</i></b>. What's the point of this? Oh, here's a monster. Look, his teeth are in his belly. This is a *insert Latin name to appear clever*. How wonderful. Now, you can eat your porridge, but just so you know, these creatures are invading New Jerusalem. What do you mean you don't care? SNAP TO, READER! SNAP TO! <br><br>I'm sorry but I can't.<br><br>Nothing fucking happens! I don't care if it's gross, I mean, I do have an history of disgust with maggots but in all honesty that's not at all what bothered me here. No. What annoyed the crap out of me is the fact that the plot felt way too simple to interest me, the whole thing punctuated with so many useless and slooooooow scenes that I struggled to keep my eyes open.<br><br>Not to mention that far from awakening my interest, the Latin names and other classics references felt somewhat pretentious to me, because they seemed completely out of phase with the simple plot.<br><br>► <b>Lack of ... <i>depth in the characterization</i></b>. Look, I love darkness. I love morally ambiguous characters. You know I do. Yet if I do like wondering what characters really think and analyzing their actions, in my opinion I haven't near enough material here to work with : everyone keeps telling me that Warthrop is complex and multi-layered, but HUH? Really, HUH? To me he's one dimensional and pretty boring, and don't get me started about the stiff and repetitive dialogues which consistently failed to convince me. <br><br>Moreover, young Will Henry lacked a voice in my opinion. I know what you're thinking, "what's her problem? It's Will's POV!" except, yes it is but no it's not. The narrator isn't 12 years-old Will Henry, but the events are told years after they originally took place. As it is, I know how old Will Henry interprets them, and if his thoughts aren't (always) uninteresting, by no means do I have any insight about what he was thinking when he was younger. Come on. <b>No 12 years-old would express an opinion in such a way.</b> None. <br><br>To me, Warthrop is pretty dry in his selfish manners and Will Henry acts like a spineless puppy. Please don't hate me, but I really didn't see anything else so far.<br><br>► <b>Lack of ... <i>emotions</i></b>. I don't mind the lack of romance, and several of my favorite books don't contain one bit of it. This being said, I need to feel at least ONE emotion - I know, I'm annoying. <br><br>What brings me to... <b>the absence of <i>fear</i></b>. Scary, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monstrumologist</a>? Monsters don't scare me. <i>Humans</i> scare me on a daily basis - when I watch the news, when I read, when I talk to random people. <i>Stupidity</i> scares me to death. Clowns do scare me, but only because Stephen King screwed up my childhood. Monsters? Nope. Grossed-out? Maybe, but never scared. <b>This book should have made my heart pound - sadly, <u>it never did</u>.</b><br><br>Anyway - <i>blablabla</i>, I didn't get it, <i>blablabla</i>, <b>I don't care is all</b>. So, okay, It's well-written. Okay. GOOD. Sadly I don't give a damn if I'm bored to death (it did escape the 1-star rating thanks to it, though).<br><br>*shrug*<br><br>I'm in the minority though, so don't let my review prevent you from reading it^^<br><br><i>For more of my reviews, please visit:</i><br><blockquote>
  <a href="https://meetthebookworld.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1487851828i/22057164._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</blockquote></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1703828525" />
    <a title="Simona B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b"><img alt="Simona B" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408893128p2/5312173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1703828525?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simona B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simona B" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b">Simona B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781481425445" href="/book/show/22540210-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=in-english">in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=2000">2000</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=fantastic-lit-and-co">fantastic-lit-and-co</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1703828525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9456003027753613406"><b>"Do you know why our race is doomed, Pellinore? Because it has fallen in love with the pleasant fiction that we are somehow above the very rules that we have determined govern everything else."</b><br/><br/>•First thing you need to know, plain and simple: rarely have I read such a <b>skillfully</b> and <b>elegantly written</b> book. And it takes a not insignificant amount of <b>versatility </b>to write a novel in 2009 and make it sound exactly<b> as if it were written in the 19th century</b> (and since I've read my fair share of 19th-ce</span>
  <span id="freeText9456003027753613406" style="display:none"><b>"Do you know why our race is doomed, Pellinore? Because it has fallen in love with the pleasant fiction that we are somehow above the very rules that we have determined govern everything else."</b><br /><br />•First thing you need to know, plain and simple: rarely have I read such a <b>skillfully</b> and <b>elegantly written</b> book. And it takes a not insignificant amount of <b>versatility </b>to write a novel in 2009 and make it sound exactly<b> as if it were written in the 19th century</b> (and since I've read my fair share of 19th-century classics, I believe I know what I am talking about). Yancey's ability left me utterly speechless, and more than once I found myself re-reading some passages, so beautiful and melodious they were. So if the author did one thing absolutely, utterly, marvelously right, it is how he set the <b>tone </b>for his story.<br /><br />•<b>The characters</b> are beyond doubt another big <b>selling point</b>. Even the little, meek Will Henry wormed his way into my heart -<i>Snap to, Will Henry!</i> Dr. Pellinor Warthrope is your classic <b>aloof, icy scientist</b> with a genius as big as his ego and not a friend in the whole world, and yet, in spite of the predictability of his figure, it's not difficult to grow to like him too, mostly because with characters like him you learn to cherish every bit of vulnerability and humanity they show, treasuring them more than you'd normally do. A special mention is due to <b>Dr. John Kearns </b>-or whatever his real name is- who managed to make me both drool and gag over a span of ten lines. Characters like him are the very reason why I will never get tired of books.<br /><br />•<b>The horror stuff</b>: I don't know if I've ever shared this secret of mine with you, but I belong to that category of people who are afraid even of the air they breathe. I can't watch a horror movie without having to give up on sleep for a week (also, I can't watch them because I find the 90% of them rather stupid. But that's another story). I can't even have a look at the <i>trailers</i>. I stop breathing when at night the wind makes the door of my room move and its hinges creak. It's not that these things <i>scare</i> me, they just give me the creeps and I can't understand why I should make myself uncomfortable without any reason. This is, basically, why I don't read horror books either. <b>I am extremely impressionable</b> and when I finish a book, I can keep thinking about it for weeks. <br />So what did <i>The Monstrumologist</i> do to my psyche?<br />Since I never felt the urge to hide somewhere, I guess the answer is nothing. I mean it positively, because it gave me <b>goosebumps</b>, yes, <b>it made my skin crawl</b>, yes, it is <b>very, very gruesome</b>, absolutely yes, but I never felt actually scared. This was totally fine for me, but some of you may expect from the book something more properly horror-ish, and either I became more tolerant after years and years of abstinence or <i>The Monstrumologist</i> doesn't aim to scare you out of your wits in the first place.<br /><br />•<b>Sometimes, I felt like my interest in the story was walking on thin ice</b>. I could not for the life of me wrap my mind around the reason why it happened, but out of five times that I re-read a passage, four were because I had adored it, and one because my attention had dwindled so much that I hadn't registered a single word. <br /><br />•Is it a mere coincidence that Will Henry and the doctor go looking for Varner <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[exactly the night before he dies <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, or does the book give an explanation and I totally missed it? Because in a 23-year period, that <i>does</i> seem a coincidence.<br /><br /><b>"For if life is a question, then I have my answer: There is no escaping it. There is no cheating fate."</b><br /><br />➽ In brief: would I recommend it? <b>Yes</b>. Will I go on with the series? <b>Yes</b>. Do I want to hug and hold tight the fabulous readers who recommended it to me (please don't kick me out of the cool nerd club)? <b>You bet I do</b>. You don't find little pearls like this every day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9456003027753613406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1003925660" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003925660" />
    <a title="Vanessa J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j"><img alt="Vanessa J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468102148p2/19590155.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1003925660?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vanessa J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vanessa J." href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416984498" href="/book/show/7171771-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of horror that are not bothered by excess of violence and gore.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=love-the-cover">love-the-cover</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=amazing-characters">amazing-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=prosegasm">prosegasm</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=mwahaha-gore">mwahaha-gore</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=all-the-feels">all-the-feels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19590155-vanessa-j?shelf=holy-shite-me-scared">holy-shite-me-scared</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1003925660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5996999497274467330"><a href='https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png' width='350' height='117' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/><b>Original review posted on <a href='https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/2015/02/27/review-the-monstrumologist/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Books With Chemistry</a> on February 2015, even though I read this for the first time on July 2014.</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>“These are the secrets I have kept. This is the trust I never betrayed.<br/><br/>But he is dead now and has been for more than forty years, the one who gave me his trust, the one for whom I kept these secrets.<br/><br/>The one who saved me … and the one who cursed me.”</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>When I first found about this book, I didn't quite want to read it. I was really hesitant to pick it up. I already had not</span>
  <span id="freeText5996999497274467330" style="display:none"><a href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" width="350" height="117" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>Original review posted on <a href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/2015/02/27/review-the-monstrumologist/" rel="nofollow noopener">Books With Chemistry</a> on February 2015, even though I read this for the first time on July 2014.</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“These are the secrets I have kept. This is the trust I never betrayed.<br><br>But he is dead now and has been for more than forty years, the one who gave me his trust, the one for whom I kept these secrets.<br><br>The one who saved me … and the one who cursed me.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>When I first found about this book, I didn't quite want to read it. I was really hesitant to pick it up. I already had not-so-high expectations for it. I mean, monsters? I'm not usually attracted by that kind of things. They don't scare me, and I think they're for children. It didn't sound like the kind of book I might enjoy, but I'm glad I finally made up my mind and gave it a try.<br><br>I don't regret at all my decision. I love this book, and have loved it since... What is it? July 2014? See? A short time. This is the kind of thing this series provoke. It's perfect in its own way. In fact, it is <i>perfect</i> for me. <b>I don't have a single complaint for it.</b> The plot, character development, writing... <i>everything</i> is done incredibly well.<br><br>So now I'm going to introduce you to the world of <i>The Monstrumologist</i>...<br><br>Monstrumology is “the study of life forms generally malevolent to humans and not recognized by science as actual organisms, specifically those considered products of myth and folklore.” The book starts with Mr. Yancey talking to someone who has some papers for him. They belonged to a now dead man who called himself by the name William James Henry. So, the book is supposed to be the diary of this Will Henry, and Rick Yancey's role was as the editor of the book.<br><br>The book's setting is in late 19th century New Jerusalem (New England, Massachusetts), and we have Will Henry (at the moment a 12-year-old boy), the orphaned assistant to Dr. Pellinore Warthrop, the monstrumologist, as the narrator of the story. In this case, they are brought a nightmarish creature that feeds himself on humans –the <i>Anthropophagi</i>– and they have to get rid of his pod before it is too late and something really bad happens.<br><br>The plot sounds too simple for what the book really is. I mean, intertwined with the hunt for the <i>Anthropophagi</i>, there is some philosophizing about human nature (my Philosophy teacher would kill me for putting the words "human" and "nature" together) in the way, but it does not bore you; or at least, it does not bore <i>me</i>. And also, there is a constant (yet not explicit in this first installment) <b>struggle between good/evil, man/monster, science/superstition,</b> and that makes me love the book even more.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“We are very much like them: indiscriminate killers, ruled by drives little acknowledged and less understood, mindlessly territorial and murderously jealous - the only significant difference being that they have yet to master our expertise in hypocrisy, the gift of our superior intellect that enables us to slaughter one another in droves, more often than not under the auspices of an approving god!”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>There is a question that is constantly analyzed throughout the book, and that one is: <b>When does man become the very thing he hunts?</b> I know that at some time, you have to become what you do. For example, if you're a hunter, you have to know how the prey thinks so as to get hold of it...and in this case, if you're a monstrumologist, then I suppose you must be one of the monstrosities you claim to hunt in order to do a good job.<br><br>The character development in the book is done in such a good way that you could <i>almost</i> feel them as real people. In fact, you <i>could</i> feel them as real people, since Mr. Yancey, being he the “editor” of Will Henry's diary, said he wanted to know who this Will Henry was, and also, because at the beginning of the book, it is stated that Will Henry had just died –at the age of one-hundred and thirty-one years.<br><br>Will Henry, as I said before, is the 12-year-old narrator of the story. He's too young to be doing the kind of job he does with the doctor, yet he does it. He's brave, innocent and loyal. His bravery, also, is realistic, for me. I know no 12-year-old kid would be able to bear the things Will Henry bears, and <i>that</i> makes him even braver. Besides, he's no fearless hero. He sometimes felt weakness inside him and sometimes fear got better of him, but that made him more realistic. He's now an old man writing down his memories, and we know his past was horrible and that he is doomed.<br><br>The doctor I mentioned before, Dr. Warthrop, is the other major character of the book. He's clever, serious, ice-cold, terribly egotistical, cruel, proud, easily exasperated, determined and cares for nothing but his work. He can spend many days and nights in his basement (laboratory) working in a case that has been brought to him, and he will not leave it unless his body cannot endure it anymore. <b>He's my favourite character in the book and the entire series.</b> I even admire him and wanna be like him some day--err, I mean, I want to be in love with and determined to my job, just like him. Oh! And I almost forgot: He loves scones.<br><br>Jack Kearns (or whatever his name is) would be the perfect definition to what a dangerous man means. If I ever thought Warthrop was inhuman, then wait to know how this man is.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“There is no morality save the morality of the moment.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Remember the question I said was analyzed throughout the series? Well, <b>this man is a perfect example of a man who is what he hunts.</b> Within his charming smile and his silky blond curls, there is madness. He can throw a convincing lie without flinching, he can use a person as bait for the <i>Anthropophagi</i> without remorse, he can humiliate and frighten the great Pellinore Warthrop, he delights in suffer, he doesn't fear death, etc. <b>It's implied that this man is the famous Jack the Ripper.</b> If that doesn't prove he's mad, then I don't know what does.<br><br>One of the things I really liked throughout the series was the Will/Warthrop relationship. As stated before, Will was an orphan. His parents died in a fire, and therefore he had no one else in life, and so, Warthrop "adopts" him as his new assistant. Warthrop is also alone in life: He's not married, has no children and all his family is already dead. Will and Warthrop have just each other. Their relationship is like that of a father and a son, and I loved it. At some point in the novel, Will says he doesn't love the doctor, nonetheless, it's really obvious he does care for him. He's loyal to him and he's always trying to impress him. <br><br>As for Warthrop, he also loved Will... in his own way. He's uncapable of showing any emotion besides anger or hatred, but he cares anyway, because he's always trying to protect Will from the dangers they get into, and if something happens to him, he feels guilty and worried.<br><br><b>And the writing is simply mesmerizing.</b> It is filled with many action-packed, fully-detailed scenes... but it is also similar to Mary Shelley's writing. This is perhaps what I admire the most in Rick Yancey's books. I've read two series by him until now, and they both have a marvelous writing. In this case, it's very dark, gothic and also poetical. His descriptions are pretty vivid and horrifying. It has a <i>big amount</i> of blood and gore, which made the book <s>more enjoyable</s> scarier. To add another praise for it, it fitted the time in which it was set.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“Our enemy is fear. Blinding, reason-killing fear. Fear consumes the truth and poisons all the evidence, leading us to false assumptions and irrational conclusions.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>Speaking about scary things... <b>Holy. Mother. Of. God.</b> This book was completely <i>creepy</i>. There were parts that made me feel uncomfortable while reading, and I was <i>never</i> alone at the house. There were some parts that made me look around me just to make sure there was anything wrong in the house, and there were others that made me stand open-mouthed and blank-faced. It was so disturbingly horrifying. I wanted to hug myself tightly most of the time.<br><br>This brings me to my next point, that is, <b>why this book is tagged as YA.</b> I don't say it is meant for adults because the hero is far too young and the theme might not call adult readers' attention, but the book is so <b>exceedingly violent and gory</b> that I often wondered if it was really aimed for YA readers. It is obviously not for MG readers, for the previously stated fact that it is far too violent for them, yet the protagonist is the age of most MG's fiction protagonists.<br><br>However, it's not only the violence that makes me question this. This book is filled with vocabulary that not everyone is going to understand, for example, names of chemical compounds, parts of the body that you don't hear about in everyday life, words in latin or greek, references to classic figures, etc. Also, <b>it has no romance</b>, which we know is ever-present in YA fiction. Well, I guess this question will remain unanswered and I will bring it to my grave still unanswered.<br><br>But the sad part about this series is that almost no one knows it. <i>The 5th Wave</i> has been read by almost every person on Earth, but strangely enough, this book is, in the majority of the cases, not even known to Rick Yancey's readers. I guess my greatest hope is that this book gets some popularity one day.<br><br>Anyway, I'll stop here. I feel I've written a Bible, so I'll finish by saying that after this reread, <i>The Monstrumologist</i> is still my favourite series of all time. In my opinion, <b>it is where Rick Yancey shows us his best.</b> If you're a fan of gothic horror, then this book might be just for you, but don't wait the entire series to be just blood, blood, blood, because it is more than that. This series starts as one thing and ends up being completely another.<br><br>So now, what are you waiting for? Go read it! And snap to!<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“Yes, my dear child, monsters are real.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="5996999497274467330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_75220447" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75220447" />
    <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"><img alt=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403239075p2/1570669.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75220447?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" class="user" itemprop="url" name=" Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)" href="/user/show/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books"> Danielle The Book Huntress (Back to the Books)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers who want a well-written, intense, but very gruesome story</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=very-dark-read">very-dark-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=oct-scare-fest-2010">oct-scare-fest-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=monster-hunters">monster-hunters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=nerd-prof-scientist-genius-geek">nerd-prof-scientist-genius-geek</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=monster-story">monster-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=not-for-the-faint-of-heart">not-for-the-faint-of-heart</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1570669-danielle-the-book-huntress-back-to-the-books?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75220447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5826179015864348224">The Monstrumologist was an unforgettable read. I can't even imagine how Rick Yancey came up with this idea. I was completely horrified many times, as I read this book. This is a young adult book, but it's not one I'd recommend lightly to just any teen, or adult for that matter. Mr. Yancey doesn't hesitate to make this story gruesome and downright stomach-churning. Due to my biological/medical background, I have a strong stomach. It came in handy when I read this book. There were scenes that I wo</span>
  <span id="freeText5826179015864348224" style="display:none">The Monstrumologist was an unforgettable read. I can't even imagine how Rick Yancey came up with this idea. I was completely horrified many times, as I read this book. This is a young adult book, but it's not one I'd recommend lightly to just any teen, or adult for that matter. Mr. Yancey doesn't hesitate to make this story gruesome and downright stomach-churning. Due to my biological/medical background, I have a strong stomach. It came in handy when I read this book. There were scenes that I would not want to watch in a movie. Sadly, I think this would be a fantastic movie, as it's very vivid and dynamic; but I would be afraid to watch it, and I think it would have to be R-rated for some of the scenes of extreme violence and gore, which is probably not ideal, since it's a young adult book. <br /><br />Thankfully, there is a strong story with a compelling set of characters to balance out the unsettling, distasteful scenes. Our protagonist is young Will Henry, an orphan who works as the assistant to an eccentric scientist. His father worked for the doctor before him, and died with his mother in a horrible fire that Will survived. Will says to himself that he never loved the Monstrumologist, but he will never leave him. It's an interesting aspect to this story, Will's devotion to this man, who barely shows him kindness or regard. For the doctor is a cold man, completely devoted to his work as a scientist who studies monsters. His skills come in handy when a group of monsters from any person's worst nightmare begin to wreak havoc on the sleepy New England town of New Jerusalem (I wondered if there was a deliberate reference to Jerusalem's Lot when I read this book). The doctor and Will embark on a quest to seek and destroy these monsters that makes for harrowing reading.<br /><br />I have to admit that this book had some moments that were hard to read. The anthropophagi are vicious predatory creatures, who view humans as a food source, and they are not sentimental in any way. What they did to the minister's family was just awful. I wanted these monsters found and destroyed, all the while fearing for the lives of Will, the doctor, and his companions. <br /><br />Gross, gory parts aside, I loved the writing in this story. Mr. Yancey establishes himself very credibly in the historical narrative of this novel. I was immersed in this world, a dark one, set in the late 19th century. In the background, one can see the significant events that would have shaped the characters, such as Darwin's work in evolution, scientific studies in eugenics, and the aftermath of the War Between the States. This is all seen through the eyes of the twelve-year-old narrator, who witnesses things that would cause a grown man to lose sleep. I could not admire Will more. He made my hero to die shelf, because this was one heck of a young man. He goes into the lion's den and into the pit of Hell numerous times to face these horrifying monsters. I could only cheer him along, my heart racing, when he comes face to face with the incredibly vicious matriarch of the group of monsters who have made New Jerusalem their preying grounds.<br /><br />In reading this book, one has to fortify herself. There are views of human nature that are just as unsettling as the monsters themselves. In fact, the man that the doctor summons to help to hunt and to kill the anthropophagi might be considered a human version of a monster himself. I admit that I found Kearns to be an interesting character. He was very lively, making me laugh a few times. However, he had absolutely no moral center, which made him a very dicey ally. He even has a line where he states that "the only morality is the morality of this moment." Yeah, that makes him a very dangerous man. Good to have against the anthropophage, but not so good if you happen to be standing in the way of his objective. Doctor Warthrop (Will's boss) was a flawed, complicated character. He came off as arrogant and uncaring in many moments, but deeply principled at his core. In fact, Kearns turns out to be a good foil to reveal the positive aspects of his character. I think the doctor cared very much for Will, but was unable to show it in the ways that we would consider most demonstrative of affection. He had so many issues with his own father, that it warped him emotionally. However, it was apparent that Will was very important to him--his companion, and the only person who cared for and about the doctor. This made their relationship very complex, and in my opinion, the core of this story.<br /><br />The Monstrumologist is a book that is quite hard to categorize and to explain in a few words. There is so much to this story. Yet, it's not one that the casual reader will enjoy. It's too dark and gruesome for that. But for an intrepid reader, there is much to admire and to appreciate in this book. After this book gets started, it doesn't wind down until it's over. It was a fascinating, powerful read, one I won't forget. Even now, it is lurking in the back of my mind like a shadow.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5826179015864348224" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_174036496" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/174036496" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2467655-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596119990p2/2467655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174036496?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/2467655-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jo by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5535721-amy-turn-the-page">Amy (Turn the Page)</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174036496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3309635460077366548"><b>A note</b>: I promise the follow is actually a review and not my application for the position of President of the Rick Yancey fan club. <br/><br/><b>
  <i>"There are times when fear is not our enemy. There are times when fear is our truest, sometimes only, friend.”</i>
</b><br/><br/><b><i>"…for only a mad man believes what every child knows to be true: </i>There are monsters that lie in wait under our beds<i>.”</i></b><br/><br/>When I was younger I never believed in monsters.<br/>I like to think it was because even then I was a little clever clogs who knew that monste</span>
  <span id="freeText3309635460077366548" style="display:none"><b>A note</b>: I promise the follow is actually a review and not my application for the position of President of the Rick Yancey fan club. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"There are times when fear is not our enemy. There are times when fear is our truest, sometimes only, friend.”</i>
</b><br /><br /><b><i>"…for only a mad man believes what every child knows to be true: </i>There are monsters that lie in wait under our beds<i>.”</i></b><br /><br />When I was younger I never believed in monsters.<br />I like to think it was because even then I was a little clever clogs who knew that monsters didn’t exist and believing in them was for babies.<br />But…um… it wasn’t.<br />Because while most children were checking in their wardrobes and under their beds for monsters, I was convinced my house was haunted and that a ghost was going to eat me and/or possess me. <br />I was a strange child.<br />My house, for the record, was only built about thirty years ago and stands at the foot of the Pennines, not on an evil cemetery. Although there <i>have</i> been rumours that, like at Hadrian’s Wall, you can hear the sounds of marching Romans on cold, quiet nights….<br />Um… what was I on about?<br />Oh yes.<br /><b>The truth: </b>I always thought that monsters were stupid. They were stupid because they could never exist.<br /><br />Huge creatures who lived under my bed with slobbering, gnashing teeth?<br /><i>Please</i>.<br />The only things under my bed were books in boxes from Ikea and an alarming amount of odd socks and Crayola pencil crayons.<br />And the Bogey man lurking in my wardrobe, ready to creep out when I was tucked up in bed?<br /><i>Whatever.</i><br />The only monster in my wardrobe is the Furby I given when I was seven and when the novelty wore off and it wouldn’t shut up, I shoved it, swaddled in an old t-shirt, into a shoe box and flung it in. <br /><br />But The Monstrumologist was not the book that I was expecting.<br />Don’t get me wrong, I was dying to read it because in my head it was a story about a cute boy running around with a cantankerous old man fighting monsters. Sounds dead lovely, yeah?<br />I thought it would be sweet and funny and I’d enjoy it but then I’d put it back on my shelf and forget about it and calmly move on to the next book. <br />Pfft.<br />Nice try, Jo. <br />Try forgetting <i>this</i> book in a hurry.<br /><br />A mixture of fantastic setting, brilliantly thought-out characters and relationships and genuinely horrifying action scenes; this book really has it all. <br /><br />This book was vile, it truly was, and I’m not squeamish at all. I love horror films, I love gory films and I’m the sick sicko who watches the needle when the nurse takes my blood.<br />But there were quite a few bits where I almost gagged. Not just pulled a face or though “Ew, that’s a bit gross.” Actually gagged. There was one in particular bit at the end and, yeeerugh, so so grim. <br />So if you’re a bit <s>of a wimp</s> on the more <i>delicate </i>end of the spectrum… there’ll probably be a few parts you’ll skip. <br />Funny I should use the phrase “a few parts”.<br />Quite apt. <br />Heh heh heh.<br /><br />But don’t let that put you off.<br />I can’t help but think if this book was ever adapted into a film (pleasepleasepleaseplease Adaption Fairy!) that, if it was <i>faithful</i> to the book, it would probably be rated as at least a 15. It is gruesome and it is macabre and it is relentless with its horror but never once does it turn gratuitous and I really loved that. <br />It’s clear that Mr Yancey knows how to tell a horror story without resorting to cheap shocks.<br /><br />But what is even clearer is that Mr Yancey can delve deep into human emotion, even the nasty bits, and portray it with startling accuracy and brutal honesty. <br />The relationships and feelings explored in this book, particularly between Will (oh Will, I think we’re going to best of friends. You’re such a badass and I will <i>always</i> pick you for my monster-fighting team) and the doctor, were so beautifully written that I made a note of almost every paragraph in this book. <br />I love the way that Yancey dealt with certain characters and situations but I can’t talk about them because of secret secrets.<br />But I’ll just say this: There are lots of monsters in this book but not all of them have fangs dripping in blood. <br /><br />I adored the prologue and the epilogue, too. <br />Don’t worry, you’re still reading one of my reviews.<br />And yep, I still hate epilogues and will continue to hate them until my dying day.<br />Except this one. <br />Usually the idea of an author adding himself as a character in his own story makes me feel a little queasy; but not this time. It added a sense of, and yes I’m aware at how stupid this will sound, realism to the whole story. <br />I could absolutely picture Mr Yancey sat in his house, pouring over Will Henry’s diaries, scribbling notes in a notepad and researching places like Deadham and Whitechapel (!!!).<br />There is a part at the end where the character Yancey describes Will Henry’s stories as “fiction” and I got angry. <br />Yes.<br />That’s right. I got <i>angry</i> at a fictionalised version of the author denouncing a fictionalised character.<br />I wanted to scream at the book: “How do <i>you</i> know if it was real or not?! It’s <u>NOT</u> fiction! I know it because I saw it! I was <i>there</i>.”<br /><br /><b>Read this book</b> with all the lights turned on.<br /><b>Read this book</b> about an hour after you’ve eaten.<br /><b>Read this book</b> with a wooden stake next to you.<br />And….um, a few grenades. <br />Better safe than sorry!<br /><br />*cough*<br /><br />Just <b>read this book.</b><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3309635460077366548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1357347740" class="readable"
