<span id="freeTextContainer10667158855052816719"><br/>EDIT: 1/20/2016: <br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1453252507ra/17803597.gif' width='500' height='267' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b> I'm so sorry for this recurring review. I did a reread with this one and I didn't even put my updated review. I was furious with the momentary disappearance of my old review and at the same time, I was hooked with Twitter.. (follow us btw, <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://twitter.com/TheWildReaders?lang=en'>https://twitter.com/TheWildReaders?la...</a> )</b><br/><br/>My review was gone and if not for Tash who gave me a link to my original review, I wouldn't have any review to this wonderful book all.<br/><br/>So here's some cookies and hot fafa for Tash:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409082942ra/10931507.gif' width='245' height='255' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Now that I'm done wi</span>
  <span id="freeText10667158855052816719" style="display:none"><br>EDIT: 1/20/2016: <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1453252507ra/17803597.gif" width="500" height="267" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b> I'm so sorry for this recurring review. I did a reread with this one and I didn't even put my updated review. I was furious with the momentary disappearance of my old review and at the same time, I was hooked with Twitter.. (follow us btw, <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://twitter.com/TheWildReaders?lang=en">https://twitter.com/TheWildReaders?la...</a> )</b><br><br>My review was gone and if not for Tash who gave me a link to my original review, I wouldn't have any review to this wonderful book all.<br><br>So here's some cookies and hot fafa for Tash:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409082942ra/10931507.gif" width="245" height="255" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now that I'm done with my shameless self promotion, here's what I think of The Monstrumologist.<br><br>Of course it's still as awesome as ever and rereading it for the 2nd time around, I was able to savor each gross and bloody moment as much as I can. But I want to stress Jack Kearns character with this one:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1453252507i/17803598.jpg" width="540" height="254" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Make no mistake, Kearns is really a villain in this book.Try pushing the main character in the cliff and feed a young lady to the monster and your status will be villain. Yes yes,the Anthropophagi are the monsters, but they were the fairy tale type of monsters. They can be defeated by a fairy tale type of hero and means.<br><br>But Kearns is different. He's the type of villain that we should really fear. He's cold, heartless, cunning and he can be found in the heart of any man. I have to admit that while I was reading the part where Kearns used the lady as a bait because that's one of the easiest way to let the monsters out, I was rooting for him.<br><br>I was ashamed because my morals were screaming at me. But deep down, I admire him. He will do any means to finish the job, morals and society be damned. When he pushed Will Henry, I hated him, but I <s>respected</s> understand why he have to do it.<br><br>So one of the reasons why The Monstrumologist is a great book is that it shows us that yes.. <i>we are all monster.</i><br><br><b>Review</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1437167656ra/15544750.gif" width="500" height="253" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show.html?id=1282901803&amp;page=2" rel="nofollow noopener">Tash's review</a> convinced me to make my own fangirl review for Warthrop-baby.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monstrumologist</a> is definitely one of the best books that I've ever read. If you're a fan of <b>monsters:</b><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726384i/15877149.gif" width="333" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I mean, <b>
  <u> scary, human-eating monsters,<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726384i/15877150._SX540_.jpg" width="540" height="360" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>a sexy, mad genius scientist,<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726384i/15877151._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>and blood and gore,</u>
</b><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726384i/15877152.gif" width="356" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>then this book/series is definitely for you.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monstrumologist</a> was told by Will Henry. He was Warthrop's <u> indispensable</u> assistant. He was only around 12 or 13 when he lived with Warthrop and all the things that he "witnessed", he wrote it in his journal which will eventually be this book.<br><br>The things that you'll read in this book is definitely guaranteed to make you vomit. Rick Yancy didn't hold back when it comes to the bloody part, so if you're a little squeamish, just make sure you have a bucket or bowl you can puke into.<br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439726384ra/15877153.gif" width="500" height="282" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b> CHARACTERS:</b>:<br><br><b>
  <u> Pellinore Warthrop:</u>
</b><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439726385ra/15877154.gif" width="500" height="260" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I love this crazy,selfish, son of a bitch. He is not the usual swoon worthy hero I usually go with, but Pellinore is one of a kind.He is very paradoxical. His ego is so big it should have its own zip code, he is cold, ruthless, kinda obtuse when it comes to feelings and he's moody.<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726385i/15877155.jpg" width="500" height="396" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>(This is my <u>Pellinore Warthrop</u>, Tash and Vane. Isn't he so fucking sexy? All the broody stare and madness is his face is visible. I want to lick him up and down.</i><br><br><b>
  <u> William James Henry</u>
</b>:<br><br>Jesus wept.<br><br>Will is the kind of character that you just want to pick up and hug till his heart and soul glowed with happiness. Will's voice is fascinating and heart wrenching. He just wants to be a child but knows he has to 'man up' because if you're living with a certain Pellinore Warthrop, you really have to toughen up.<br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726385i/15877156.jpg" width="300" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>(Note: Saw this photo and I can't help casting them as Warthrop and Will Henry)</i><br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439726385i/15877157.jpg" width="540" height="540" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><b>
  <u> John Kearns</u>
</b><br><br>What to say about this character? He's charismatic and he's ruthless. He does have a shady moral and he's loves upsetting Warthrop. He's the kind of person who makes other people wants to punch him.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1439726385ra/15877158.gif" width="500" height="260" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monstrumologist</a> is the type of book that I wouldn't hesitate to recommend. <br><br>Here are the reason why you should buy a copy of this book and read it:<br><br>The suspense is top-notch.<br>The book has this thing called "character development".<br><b>Pellinore Warthrop.</b> ('Nuff said.)<br>No bullshits in this book.<br>Scary monsters.<br>Monster hunting. <br>And this book shows us that humans are sometimes more terrifying than the things that we hunt.<br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1435713525ra/15372966.gif" width="500" height="281" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="10667158855052816719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1262353135" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262353135" />
    <a title="Evelyn (devours and digests words)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words"><img alt="Evelyn (devours and digests words)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556809829p2/38658747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1262353135?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evelyn (devours and digests words)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evelyn (devours and digests words)" href="/user/show/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words">Evelyn (devours and digests words)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8161464-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Non-Squeamish people who are a fan of anything that goes bump in the night</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Evelyn (devours and digests words) by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=recommended-to-me">recommended-to-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=writing-i-would-love-to-imitate">writing-i-would-love-to-imitate</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=tortured-hero">tortured-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=fantastical">fantastical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=welcome-to-the-dark-side">welcome-to-the-dark-side</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=series-i-actually-like">series-i-actually-like</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38658747-evelyn-devours-and-digests-words?shelf=cover-beauts">cover-beauts</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262353135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12111336756627657195"><i>'Yes, my dear child, monsters are real. I happen to have one hanging in my basement.' </i><br/><br/>This should have come with a warning that says <b>READER DISCRETION IS ADVISED</b> printed in <b>bold</b> and stamped on the very first page.<br/><br/><i>Because man, this was hella bloody. I kid you not.</i><br/><br/>See, I have a not-so-secret part of me that digs violence and gore in books and movies, so I was practically foaming in the mouth when I learned (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003925660?book_show_action=false' rel='nofollow noopener'>through Vane's kick-ass lengthy review</a>) that the story is about chomping, man-eating monste</span>
  <span id="freeText12111336756627657195" style="display:none"><i>'Yes, my dear child, monsters are real. I happen to have one hanging in my basement.' </i><br><br>This should have come with a warning that says <b>READER DISCRETION IS ADVISED</b> printed in <b>bold</b> and stamped on the very first page.<br><br><i>Because man, this was hella bloody. I kid you not.</i><br><br>See, I have a not-so-secret part of me that digs violence and gore in books and movies, so I was practically foaming in the mouth when I learned (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1003925660?book_show_action=false" rel="nofollow noopener">through Vane's kick-ass lengthy review</a>) that the story is about chomping, man-eating monsters.<br><br>So obviously I expect a lot of bloody entrails flying everywhere.<br><br><i> Splatter! Splatter!</i><br><br>The entire storyline is uniquely presented in the form of a diary entry - split in 3 volumes that had once belonged to an old man called Will Henry who claimed to be born in the late 19th century. When the diaries are uncovered it is learned that over more than a hundred years ago, a 12-year-old Will Henry used to serve as an apprentice for Doctor Pellinore Warthrop who practiced Monstrumology - the study of monsters. <i>The Anthropophagi</i> were terrorising New Jerusalem. Through Warthrop's research, they found out that these monsters hid out in graveyards tearing apart fresh corpses while planting their seed in dead female wombs. When they took out a whole family. Serious actions were taken. <i>The Anthrophophagi</i> must be wiped out for good before every single souls in town end up in their bellies.<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monstrumologist</a> may be all about defeating the man-eating beasts but it also delve deeper into something else other than that. Philosophy. <b>Not only is the story laced with gore, it's also layered with cynicism that makes you stare into space and ponder over the meaning of it all.</b><br><br><blockquote>'There are monsters like the <i>Anthropophagi</i>, and then there are the monsters of a more banal bent.'</blockquote><br><br><b>This books made me question human nature and urged me to compare man from beast.</b><br><br><blockquote>'We are very much like them: indiscrimate killers, ruled by drives little acknowledged and less understood, mindlessly territorial and murderously jealous.'</blockquote><br><br>Who are the real monsters here especially when we are not so different from them?<br><br>                 * * *<br><br>Not only did Rick Yancey not shy away from going overboard with his blood splattered descriptions, both his writing style and proses are absolutely beautiful. Flowery proses usually make my eyes droop but in this case, I soaked in every single words, and I never even feel the impulse to skim! The tone is  strangely soothing and it just really calms me down when I read which is saying something because I shouldn't even be calm when crazy stuffs keep happening left and right!<br><br>I perked up every time Warthrop explains about the anatomy of the Anthropophagi to Will Henry. I love the gross details and the complication that comes with the observations. Reading through all that was like being seated in a lecture hall. I love Biology and eventhough the whole Science stuffs in the book is made up, it's still attention-grabbing and its what made the book most interesting to read.  I'd say that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6457229.The_Monstrumologist__The_Monstrumologist___1_" title="The Monstrumologist (The Monstrumologist, #1) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Monstrumologist</a> has taught me a lot <s>about monsters</s> but that would be crazy. I can't exactly use the stuffs in here for educational purposes, can I? And it's not as if the <i>Anthrophophagi</i> exists.<br><br>Now if we put aside the yummy gore, the intricate writing, the deep philosophy and the non-mind-exploding scientific stuffs. We'd still have the memorable characterization.<br><br><b>The stand out characters are most definitely Dr. Pellinore Warthrop himself</b> (You will best remember him with his constant commands of snap to's and his grumpy-frumpy nature) <b>and Cory Kearns</b> (who can't seem to decide what he want for his own name.)<br><br><blockquote> "What do you mean, Cory or Kearns or whatever your blasted name is?" <br><br>"It's Cory; I thought I made it quite clear."<br><br>"I don't care if its John Jacob Jingleheimer Schmidt!" </blockquote><br><br><b>Do not think this is a grim book. </b>Yes, it can be cynical and dark at times and there are the occasional bloodshed and death. But it really isn't all that depressing. <b>There was humour in it.</b>Thanks to Kearns and his flamboyant nature which then lead me to finding it hard to decide whether I like Warthrop or Kearns more.<br><br>I admire Warthrop for his complexity, honesty, and discipline manner. He can be stone cold to everyone even to Will Henry who he 'adopted' after the boy's parents died but as the story progress so does Warthrop's characterization. Despite his cold demeanour, he has a caring, soft side. When he admitted his mistake and showed a shred of compassion I nearly fainted from swooning.<br><br><blockquote>"I may be a fool and a terrible scientist, blinded by ambition and pride to the most obvious truths, but one thing I am <i>not</i> is a liar." </blockquote><br><br>Meanhile Kearns is a special case. He's the kind of person I'd mistaken for funny and charming with the devil's sense of humour. When the situations turn grim, he was the one who adds laughter and ease the tension. However underneath it all, he is truly despicable and has a cynical point of view toward mankind. He'd never hesitate to use a live victim as the <i> Anthropophagi's </i> bait! He comes so close to psychotic that I can't help but be in extreme awe. The dude is crazy but you can't help but admire him at the same time.<br><br>I cannot say much for Will Henry except that he's innocent, loyal and humble. He's too thick for me to be able to connect properly though. But since this is only the first book in a series, I can be able to pardon that and hope his characterization will be more solid in the following books to come.<br><br>To be frank, the main plot wasn't all that mind-blowing as I'd expected it would be. But despite the overused storyline, I still liked this. <b>A book about anything that goes bump in the night is my favoured kind </b>and there were more positive points than negative ones. So I decided to turn off the mean, petty reviewer in me and give this a solid 4 stars.<br><br><i>I would also love to thank my friend and fellow co-blogger <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/19590155-vane" rel="nofollow noopener">Vane</a> for introducing me to this world of monsters and other freakies to come.</i><br><br><br><a href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" width="350" height="117" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="12111336756627657195" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1409848618" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1409848618" />
    <a title="Masooma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26677122-masooma"><img alt="Masooma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455366226p2/26677122.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1409848618?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Masooma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Masooma" href="/user/show/26677122-masooma">Masooma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Masooma by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/19590155-vanessa-j">Vanessa J.</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1409848618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11044788004542527302">4.5 stars<br/><br/><b>Like the lion, it uses its claws as its primary form of attack, but unlike the large mammalian predators, it does not attempt to kill its prey before it begins to feed. More like the shark or an insect, the Anthropophagus prefers living flesh.</b><br/><br/>The Anthropophagus are horrendous creatures who live and thrive on human meat. They are African natives but somehow, mysteriously, have reached New Jerusalem where our hero, Will Henry resides and serves in the Harrington Lane. In this house, also </span>
  <span id="freeText11044788004542527302" style="display:none">4.5 stars<br><br><b>Like the lion, it uses its claws as its primary form of attack, but unlike the large mammalian predators, it does not attempt to kill its prey before it begins to feed. More like the shark or an insect, the Anthropophagus prefers living flesh.</b><br><br>The Anthropophagus are horrendous creatures who live and thrive on human meat. They are African natives but somehow, mysteriously, have reached New Jerusalem where our hero, Will Henry resides and serves in the Harrington Lane. In this house, also lives a monstrumologist, a monster hunter who is fascinated by these creatures and pursues learning more and more about them secretly although now he can't keep it buried any more for the Anthropophagus have struck their first victim.<br><br>The Monstrumologist is strictly a book for the strong-hearted who are capable of digesting horrible monsters, disgusting diseases, gruesome details and high-level suspense. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3377941.Rick_Yancey" title="Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener">Rick Yancey</a> has done a phenomenal job in making the book enticing from the first page itself and delivering a fully thrill-packed novel ahead.<br><br>The writing is mesmerizing. It suits the background of the late nineteenth century in which the story is set. Some expressions are so beautiful this I was left speechless such as;<br><br><b>My presence faded from his consciousness as light fades from the autumnal sky</b><br><br><b>The sound of their voices crashing against the plaster like storm surges smashing against a seawall</b><br><br>Unfortunately, such phrases abound in the beginning and gradually reduce as the book proceeds.<br><br>The details are horrific. Every scene is lucid. Even a single noise in the kitchen can raise the hair on the nape of the neck.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1444142025ra/16458812.gif" width="499" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>It feels like the author has mastered the art of scene and suspense development. He satiates all of one's senses.<br><br><b>I heard my mother cry out. A shadow appeared in my doorway, and the room was filled with a horrible stench that closed by throat I could not breathe. The shadow filled the doorway... huge and headless... huffing and sniffing like a hog. I was paralyzed.</b><br><br>He gives such details that you'd keep checking what's behind your back and at every turn of the events, your heart beat would flutter.<br><br>The character development is also exceptional. Will Henry, the narrator is the assistant of the monstrumologist- Dr.Pellinore. He is an observant assistant who keeps tucking away important information snippets in the back drawer of his mind and then bringing it the fore when the need arises.<br>He's highly courageous and his bravery is superb considering Will is only TWELVE YEARS OLD. For a little child, he sees and bears way too much and has a personality which would strike a cord or two of each of his readers' heart.<br><br>The mad monstrumologist is consumed whole by his scientific ambition and passion as a soldier for science. He's like the typical, crazy scientist, unaware of his dwindling diet, messed-up hair and untidy clothing. Most of the times, he's flummoxed when it comes to normal conversations. But what is the best is the slow revelation of his character's emotions which come to the light only slightly but satisfactorily.<br><br>All characters including these two are fully realistic and flawed, submerged in questions of conscience and morals, confused, shocked and terrified like any other human in such circumstances.<br><br>I am all praise for this novel and I'd recommend it to anyone who loves this genre, there's a lot to talk about this one but at the moment I've to check if there is anything under my bed!<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="11044788004542527302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5323875317682032244"><b>3.5</b><br/><br/><a href='http://postimage.org/' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443506341i/16370250._SX540_.jpg' alt='frosch' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><a target='_blank' href='http://postimage.org/' rel='nofollow noopener'>adult image</a><br/><br/><br/><br/>I agree with Frosch. This book is yucky. By yucky, I mean totally gross and stomach-churning. With the book's graphic and vivid descriptions of the gory and bloody scenes, I can always hear my stomach complaining and twisting not of hunger but of disgust. If it can speak I know my stomach will beg me to stop reading this book.<br/><br/>The Monstrumologist is a great book. I know I got bored with the first half of this book because the writing and I didn't get so well but I thought it was</span>
  <span id="freeText5323875317682032244" style="display:none"><b>3.5</b><br><br><a href="http://postimage.org/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443506341i/16370250._SX540_.jpg" alt="frosch" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><a target="_blank" href="http://postimage.org/" rel="nofollow noopener">adult image</a><br><br><br><br>I agree with Frosch. This book is yucky. By yucky, I mean totally gross and stomach-churning. With the book's graphic and vivid descriptions of the gory and bloody scenes, I can always hear my stomach complaining and twisting not of hunger but of disgust. If it can speak I know my stomach will beg me to stop reading this book.<br><br>The Monstrumologist is a great book. I know I got bored with the first half of this book because the writing and I didn't get so well but I thought it was good all the same. When a book made me think and feel things and imparted knowledge and stirred something in me (e.g. textbooks), I labeled it good even I had difficult times reading it.<br><br>Besides, the second half redeemed itself and rectified my boredom to anticipation and excitement. And I thanked Dr. Kearns and his odd personality for that. I enjoyed his humor, oddity and being honest and brutal.(if that's the right term for it). I really can't describe his character but I can say that I love and hate him at the same time.<br><br>I also love the major characters Pellinore Warthrop and Will Henry, especially, when the two are talking to each other. I found their conversations funny and it lessen my boredom away after a long narrative of nosebleed English words and phrases. <br><br>At first, I can't connect with Warthrop but in the end, I realized how he truly cares with Will Henry. I felt happy about it, I swear. The story of his past and the longing for his father revealed his true self that he won me over.<br><br>Nonetheless, I admired Will James Henry for he is a brave, well-mannered kid on his time. I admired his perception, patience and thoughts. He looked like a monotonous, boring kid but he saw things and thought about them and it made the difference. Though, sometimes, I got bored with his narrative. <br><br>Finally, I love love the last chapter and the epilogue. I can feel that it is hinting something interesting, mysterious. I just hope I won't be disappointed.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_128659337" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128659337" />
    <a title="Brigid ✩" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/886144-brigid"><img alt="Brigid ✩" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581869052p2/886144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128659337?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brigid ✩" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brigid ✩" href="/user/show/886144-brigid">Brigid ✩</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=steampunk">steampunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/886144-brigid?shelf=yes-yes-yes">yes-yes-yes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128659337" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17996354760129931860">This book was quite the pleasant surprise.<br/><br/>I didn't really know what to expect when I first picked it up. I had heard of it a couple of times before––and maybe I'd seen it at the library and previously thought about reading it. I just recalled being told that this book was really graphic and frightening. So I was just like, "Well, here goes nothing!"<br/><br/>Within about twenty or thirty pages, I was already feeling a bit sick to my stomach. Let's just say, this book just got right into the gory stuff. An</span>
  <span id="freeText17996354760129931860" style="display:none">This book was quite the pleasant surprise.<br><br>I didn't really know what to expect when I first picked it up. I had heard of it a couple of times before––and maybe I'd seen it at the library and previously thought about reading it. I just recalled being told that this book was really graphic and frightening. So I was just like, "Well, here goes nothing!"<br><br>Within about twenty or thirty pages, I was already feeling a bit sick to my stomach. Let's just say, this book just got right into the gory stuff. And since I'm kind of a squeamish person, at first I really didn't think I was going to enjoy this. But as I got further into the story, I found myself more and more wrapped up in it. And ultimately, I actually loved it. (Hence, the five stars.) <br><br>But let's go a little more into detail ...<br><br>The setting of the story is a bit ambiguous––but we know it takes place in the late 1800's in New England (possibly in or somewhere around Massachusetts–-my home state, woohoo!). Our protagonist is 12-year-old Will Henry, who is an assistant/apprentice to Pellinore Warthrop––aka the doctor, or the monstrumologist. Will is an orphan, taken in by the doctor after the gruesome death of Will's parents. (Will's father also worked for the doctor.)<br><br>Monstrumology is the scientific study and the hunting of monsters. In this particular case, Will and Dr. Warthrop are studying the sudden outbreak of a monster called the <i>Anthropophagi</i>––which is a somewhat humanlike creature, except it has no head, and it has eyes in its shoulders and a mouth for a stomach. Oh, and it eats people of course.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380222758ra/301224.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Bahaha, okay. No more "Troll 2" jokes, I promise.<br><br>Anyway, I was kind of surprised to see that this book has kind of a low rating on Goodreads (I mean, considering a lot of really awful books have averages of like 4.5 and this book has a 3.9). But, I also realize that this book might not appeal to the majority of the YA audience, for several reasons:<br><br>1. There is no romance.<br><br>2. In fact, there are no major female characters in this book at all. Probably only three or four women show up in the entire story, and they're very, very minor characters. <br><br>3. There's a lot of extreme and graphic violence. (I mean, if you think The Hunger Games is violent for a YA book, just ... HAHAHAHAHAHAHA. You have no idea.)<br><br>4. The main character is quite young. He's not even a teenager himself. If it weren't for all the gore and whatnot, the book would probably be middle grade solely based on the protagonist's age. <br><br>5. The writing is kind of in a Gothic/Romantic style––think along the lines of Mary Shelley or Edgar Allan Poe. So, probably not something that a lot of younger readers are used to, or can easily understand. I see a lot of negative reviews complaining that the writing is too old-fashioned and whatnot. Although, since it's set more than a hundred years ago, I think the style makes sense ... but everyone's entitled to their opinion and all. And if you don't like that type of classic style, this book is probably not going to be your thing.<br><br>So anyway, I see how this book is difficult to put into a genre. I guess it's the type of book that's just categorized as YA because it's too mature to be for children, but the protagonist is also very young so you can't really aim it at adults. But, I think there are a lot of things about this book that a lot of younger teenage readers might not enjoy. I mean, if I read this book when I was 13 or so, I probably would have hated it. If I had read it back then, I don't think I would have understood the influences behind it and it just wouldn't have been as interesting to me. Now, being almost an adult, I can see what might have inspired Rick Yancey.<br><br>As I've already mentioned, the style seemed to have a lot of Gothic and Romantic influence. The story is full of gloom and horror and mystery, and of course is full of supernatural elements. Yancey also writes in great detail, creating a lot of horrific and unforgettable images.<br><br>And that brings me to the writing. Oh, how I love the writing in this book. I mean ... WOW. Mr. Yancey is just terrific at detailed imagery. I could see everything so clearly. And yes, most of it was really frightening and disgusting, and caused me to make faces like this:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380340960ra/697223.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But it takes a lot of skill to create such memorable images!<br><br>The whole thing sort of reminded me of reading something Shelley/Poe-esque, crossed with Sherlock Holmes or something. Although, I think that might just be because Dr. Warthrop reminded me a lot of Sherlock––in love with his work but usually unfeeling towards other people, brilliant but also kind of insane, etc. Ahhh. I was kind of in love with him. But maybe that's just because I kept picturing him looking like Benedict Cumberbatch ...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380340960i/697224._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>MMMM.<br><br>Uh, where was I? Oh yeah.<br><br>So, I guess that brings me to the characterization. If you haven't gathered it already, Warthrop was my favorite character. I liked his Sherlock vibe. He could be intense, yet he could also be hilarious. He could act completely unfeeling, yet there were a lot of subtle moments when the reader could glimpse how much he cared about Will.<br><br>As for Will, I wouldn't say he was the best protagonist in the world, although he was likable enough. I think what I especially appreciated about him was that he acted his age––that despite all the horrible things he had gone though, he would still feel horrified or sickened at the appropriate moments. He still cried or threw up or something, if things got really bad ... which I thought was realistic. And his attitude toward the doctor is quite interesting. Obviously, he has mixed feelings. Warthrop is kind of insane and can be very insensitive––not to mention, he might have caused the death of Will's parents––yet he is the only thing Will has left. So, the whole relationship is very complex and intriguing.<br><br>Kearns was also a fascinating character. Almost kind of a Moriarty in this situation––just as brilliant as Warthrop, but more ... evil. He was an interesting comparison to Warthrop, and provided the reader with a lot of questions. Such as ... if you spend so much time studying such horrible creatures, how long will it take before you start to think like the monster you're hunting? Hmmm.<br><br>The plot itself was very exciting, and often I would find the book hard to put down.<br><br>I would be reading like:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380340960ra/697225.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And then things would be getting creepy and I'd be like:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380340960ra/697226.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And I would just know something horrible was going to happen and I'd be like:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380340960ra/697227.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And then MONSTERS WOULD COME OUT AND RIP PEOPLE TO SHREDS AND STUFF, AND I'D BE LIKE:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380222758ra/478317.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>... And then it would be over and I'd just be left sitting there like:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380340960ra/697228.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So yeah, I think that about sums it up. It was quite the emotional roller coaster. But altogether, it was just awesome.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380222758ra/297841.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And now I'm reading the sequel with very high expectations. Woohoo!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17996354760129931860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1084324103" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1084324103" />
    <a title="Mia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23106940-mia"><img alt="Mia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600049623p2/23106940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1084324103?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mia" href="/user/show/23106940-mia">Mia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416984498" href="/book/show/7171771-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=psychopaths-are-fun">psychopaths-are-fun</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23106940-mia?shelf=dark-grim-scary">dark-grim-scary</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1084324103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17407537606170493606"><i>"I had a thought, dear, however scary<br/>About that night, the bugs and the dirt<br/>Why were you digging?<br/>What did you bury<br/>Before those hands pulled me from the earth?<br/><br/>I will not ask you where you came from,<br/>I will not ask and neither should you."</i><br/>-Hozier, 'Like Real People Do'<br/><br/>The doctor is <i>in!</i><br/><br/>What to say about this book? I have a lot to say, actually, but right now I have that wonderful feeling of total satisfaction that you get from finishing a book that had all the right stuff in it- plot, writing, chara</span>
  <span id="freeText17407537606170493606" style="display:none"><i>"I had a thought, dear, however scary<br>About that night, the bugs and the dirt<br>Why were you digging?<br>What did you bury<br>Before those hands pulled me from the earth?<br><br>I will not ask you where you came from,<br>I will not ask and neither should you."</i><br>-Hozier, 'Like Real People Do'<br><br>The doctor is <i>in!</i><br><br>What to say about this book? I have a lot to say, actually, but right now I have that wonderful feeling of total satisfaction that you get from finishing a book that had all the right stuff in it- plot, writing, characters, structure- the whole bloody, shocking, fantastic, gory enchilada. Because there's so much to talk about, I'll separate this review into sections to spare you the horror of reading through an enormous wicket of blocks of text.<br><br><b>
  <i>Synopsis</i>
</b><br><br>The book opens in June of 2007, when Yancey, the author, is commissioned by the director of a retirement home to leaf through the journals of a resident by the name of William James Henry. Will, who claimed to have been born in 1876, died a few days before, and these were the last of his possessions, so the director asks Mr Yancey to read through them and search for any identifying details as to Will's identity, or specifications to whom the journals should be passed on, as nobody has claimed Will as their kin. Like any self-respecting author confronted with several folios of mysterious, worn notebooks, Yancey accepts the offer. What follows are the first three folios of Will's journals.<br><br>The rest of the book is set in 1888, New England, with a twelve-year-old Will Henry as the protagonist and narrator, whose parents died a year ago in a fire and who, ever since, has lived with Pellinore Warthrop, a cold and mysterious doctor of even an more mysterious profession- monstrumology. What happens in Will's second year under Warthrop's care is an exceedingly strange and gruesome affair concerning bloodthirsty monsters and more than a few morally bankrupt supporting characters. We'll travel with Will to horrifying sanatoriums, into misty graveyards and through dank, pitch-dark tunnels where it becomes ever more difficult to believe Dr Warthrop's saying that fear is the ultimate enemy. The claustrophobia and insanity, the smell of smoke and decay, monsters in forms both familiar and foreign, blood and sinew and bones- these are the things that distinguish <i>The Monstrumologist</i>. And I loved every damn second of it.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1442451362ra/16236267.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>William James Henry</i>
</b><br><br>After Will's parents died when his house burned down- the grisly details of which are revealed later on- his father's employer, one Doctor Pellinore Warthrop, takes him in. It's immensely lonely for Will living at the Warthrop residence on 425 Harrington Lane- it would be for anyone, even more so a twelve-year-old boy thrown into new and terrifying circumstances. His parents' deaths were so sudden that he's now thrust into the demanding job of being Warthrop's assistant, and all that it entails: being woken up late at night for whatever Warthrop needs, assisting with necropsies, cooking, cleaning, and generally helping with the doctor's obscure calling.<br><br>Not to fear, though- Will is a tough little orphan. He sees things that would send grown adults cowering into corners, monsters that everyone has always assumed only live in our darkest nightmares. It's a solitary way to live, and despite Warthrop's presence, Will is usually on his own. <br><blockquote>I was not unused to this odd isolation in his company, but had yet to become accustomed to the effect it had upon me: There is no loneliness more profound, in my experience, than being ignored by one's sole companion in life. Whole days would pass with nary a word from him, even as we supped together or worked side by side in the laboratory or took our evening constitutional along Harrington Lane. When he did speak to me, it was rarely to engage me in conversation; rather, our roles were rigidly defined. His was to speak; mine was to pay attention. He held forth; I listened. He: the orator; I: the audience. I had learned quickly not to speak unless spoken to; to obey any command instantly and without question, no matter how mystifying or seemingly absurd; to stand ready, as it were, as a good soldier dedicating his sacred honor to a worthy cause, though it was the rare instance when I understood precisely what that cause might be.</blockquote><br><b>
  <i>Pellinore Warthrop</i>
</b><br><br>As much as I loved little Will Henry, Warthrop was, without a doubt, my favourite character. He is the eponymous monstrumologist- he studies, and, if necessary, hunts "life forms generally malevolent to humans and not recognized by science as actual organisms, specifically those considered products of myth and folklore." He takes this profession seriously- his work is his life, and he often spends days at a time without eating or sleeping, sustained only on black tea, if he has a lead on one of these malevolent organisms. <br><br>I was expecting a pretty cut-and-dried mad scientist- obsessed with science, has some sort of sinister master plan or agenda for his research (taking over the world maybe?), ruthless, willing and able to lie and cheat and steal to achieve his ends. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442452843i/16236650._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>What I got instead was Doctor Warthrop.<br><br>He's quite earnest- he is obsessed with monstrumology, but he also has a regard for human life, and he isn't quite as ruthless as he may seem at first. He's tough and wickedly smart, but he's as stubborn and uncooperative and egotistical as they come. I think Warthrop described himself best when he said:<br><blockquote>"We are slaves, all of us, Will Henry. Some are slaves to fear. Others are slaves to reason- or base desire. It is our lot to be slaves, Will Henry, and the question must be to what shall we owe our indenture? Will it be to truth or to falsehood, hope or despair, light or darkness? I choose to serve the light, even though that bondage often lies in darkness."</blockquote><br>Warthrop is such a fascinating and complex character, and puzzling together his intentions and motivations is an equally fascinating exercise. Also, how awesome of a name is Pellinore Warthrop?!<br><br><b>
  <i>The Monsters</i>
</b><br><br>These are monsters done <i>right!</i> They aren't the monsters we've become accustomed to seeing- they don't brood about their fate, they don't pretend to be human, they don't pine for humanity or love or release from their bestial confines. <br><br>They are <i>ferocious</i>.<br>They have <i>no heads</i>.<br>They eat humans <i>alive</i>.<br>They have a bite-force of <i>two thousand pounds.</i><br><br>They are <i>Anthropophagi</i>.<br><br>They are monsters whose mouths are gaping pits of razor-sharp teeth set into the middle of their torso, who feast upon human flesh with the unbridled ferocity of a Tasmanian devil. Think this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442452843i/16236651.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Combined with this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1442452843i/16236652._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>To form something along the lines of this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439680321i/15871561._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>...And you've pretty much got an Anthropophagus. They're the villains of this story, the monsters to be exterminated, and they are really worthy monsters. Horrifying. <br><br><br><b>
  <i>Plot</i>
</b><br><br>There are two main plotlines working in tandem in this book: 1) The mystery of how the Anthropophagi pod got to America in the first place, and 2) The dilemma of how to kill them all, when their strength is far superior to humans'. I'm purposely keeping it vague here because I don't want to give you any hints to either of the answers to these questions.<br><br>You've probably noticed that I gave this book four stars. Make no mistake, I loved it- but since lying is the worst kind of buffoonery I must confess that I did not find the plot five-star worthy. It's pretty linear, which is fine because it's executed well and there are a fair amount of twists, but I kind of wish there was more going on. I love a book that can juggle several plots and character arcs without making them too jumbled or confusing, books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1232.The_Shadow_of_the_Wind__The_Cemetery_of_Forgotten_Books___1_" title="The Shadow of the Wind (The Cemetery of Forgotten Books, #1) by Carlos Ruiz Zafón" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Shadow of the Wind</a>. <i>The Monstrumologist</i> had five star potential, no doubt, but the plot was rather simple, and even though it was only .5 stars away from being a five, I round my reviews down because I am a cold-hearted harpy.<br><br><br><b>
  <i>Writing</i>
</b><br><br>Apart from the extremely well fleshed-out characters, Rick Yancey's writing is really what makes this book shine. He masters the gothic Victorian tone and aces the setting, and he was able pull off the feeling of a 100+ year-old man looking back on experiences he had when he was 12. The sentences are gorgeous, the metaphors complex and beautiful, and somehow it never feels purple or overwrought. It would be so easy to venture into cheesy or cliché territory here, but Yancey deftly avoids the line separating high-quality from trying-too-hard. He also seems to be very fond of alliteration- there's a lot of it in the book- which I actually quite liked. <br><br>Beware, my more sensitive friends- there is a sizable amount of gore and nastiness within these pages, so read at your own risk. Some scenes even feature pus, that most repulsive of bodily fluids. But, to me, it was never gratuitous, and in places it really helped me to step into the book, to feel the revulsion and shock and horror that Will felt, the horror of realising that all the nightmares of things waiting in the dark were not fabrications at all. Take, for instance, the sanatorium scene with Hezekiah Varner- for the last three pages I didn't even realise I was holding my breath until my vision started to swim.<br><br><i>"Fresh from the fields, all fetor and fertile, <br>It's bloody and raw, but I swear it is sweet."</i> -Hozier, 'Angel of Small Death &amp; the Codeine Scene'<br><br><i>
  <b>Closing Thoughts</b>
</i><br><br>While the marketing for <i>The Monstrumologist</i> really sells it short, in my opinion- this is most definitely NOT Young Adult Fiction!- the book remains a remarkably well-written and thoroughly entertaining story. The characters are not only relatable and interesting, but they ask important questions, something I wish more books would do. Questions like, when do the ends justify the means? Is one life taken worth one life saved? What happens when a scientist get to close to the subject? Finally, how are humans really different from all other animals, and will the belief that we have conquered nature's viciousness be our downfall?<br><br>Will Henry and Warthrop's relationship was wonderful, their seemingly one-sided conversations were hilarious, and the way their roles grew and changed and became less defined felt natural and was really quite touching. <br><br>Sorry if this review is long and dry and doesn't do the book justice, I am very tired but I felt the need to review this because it is such an incredibly absorbing book. I'm looking forward to tearing through <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7775755.The_Curse_of_the_Wendigo__The_Monstrumologist___2_" title="The Curse of the Wendigo (The Monstrumologist, #2) by Rick Yancey" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Curse of the Wendigo</a> next!<br><br><br>Also: I must send out my heartfelt thanks to the lovely <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/19590155-vane-j" rel="nofollow noopener">Vane</a>. Without her prodding me to finally pick this book up, I've no doubt it would have just sat on my TBR shelf for months, perhaps years. I blame her for my budding addiction to this series. Also, check out <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1282901803?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Tash's</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1262353135?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Evelyn's </a> great reviews of this book, two other friends who assisted in the creation of my obsession.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17407537606170493606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1070721478" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1070721478" />
    <a title="Liz Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet"><img alt="Liz Janet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574021381p2/5114433.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1070721478?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz Janet" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet">Liz Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781416984498" href="/book/show/7171771-the-monstrumologist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5114433-liz-janet?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1070721478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12827104657075302260">This book is considered a young adult horror novel, and in 2010 it became an honor book of the Printz Award, so it is easy to say that it’s literary excellence.<br/><br/><i>“He knew the truth. Yes, my dear child, he would undoubtedly tell a terrified toddler tremulously seeking succor, monsters are real. I happen to have one hanging in my basement.”</i><br/><br/>It all begins when a grave robber discovers an Anthropophagi (monster) with an eaten girl, and Will Henry and the Doctor must begin to uncover what has happened a</span>
  <span id="freeText12827104657075302260" style="display:none">This book is considered a young adult horror novel, and in 2010 it became an honor book of the Printz Award, so it is easy to say that it’s literary excellence.<br /><br /><i>“He knew the truth. Yes, my dear child, he would undoubtedly tell a terrified toddler tremulously seeking succor, monsters are real. I happen to have one hanging in my basement.”</i><br /><br />It all begins when a grave robber discovers an Anthropophagi (monster) with an eaten girl, and Will Henry and the Doctor must begin to uncover what has happened and how to stop what is to come.<br /><br /><i>“Perhaps that is our doom, our human curse, to never really know one another. We erect edifices in our minds about the flimsy framework of word and deed, mere totems of the true person, who, like the gods to whom the temples were built, remains hidden. We understand our own construct; we know our own theory; we love our own fabrication. Still … does the artifice of our affection make our love any less real?” </i><br /><br />The main character is called Will Henry, and he is a child of around 12-14, he is orphaned as his father and mother died in a fire, and he was the taken by his father’s boss, Dr. Warthrop. Warthrop is a ‘mad scientist,” however he has clarity, so he knows that what he is doing is not okay, but he does it anyways. We don’t really get to know the doctor’s true intentions with Will Henry until much later in the book, but it was intriguing to say the least. If you are thinking Will Henry is too young to be doing what he is doing as assistant to this sociopath/psychopath, the answer is yes, but what else can he do, when the man cannot even tolerate child-like behaviour. We must remember that no matter how brave Will Henry is in the presence of the horrors he endures, he is also a child, and albeit brave, he is not fearless, and unlike Warthrop, he has a conscience for now. The doctor is cold, and harsh, cruel and very proud, but he is nothing compared to another character named Jack Kearns, unlike the doctor who knows what he is doing is wrong, this guy has no sense of morality or an ethic code, I could even go as far as to say he is not human, but one of the monsters the monstrumologist should study. This “man” is not afraid of death, he has not qualms about using a person as bait, he has no problems with lying. <br /><br />This is a novel about good and evil, how science and folklore come together, and the big one, when does a man become the monster he hunts. I have seen some people comparing it to Frankenstein by Mary Shelley, and although I don’t necessarily agree, I can see the comparisons drawn between the novels. They are both Gothic horrors, with beautiful prose, and layered characters, while the book itself poses many questions about the human condition, who we are, and what makes us. <br /><br /><i>“There are times when fear is not our enemy. There are times when fear is our truest, sometimes only, friend.”</i><br /><br /><b>Guillermo Del Toro or Tim Burton could do an amazing film of this book, and I would watch the hell out of it.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12827104657075302260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Neal Shusterman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5092518-neal-shusterman"><img alt="Neal Shusterman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442449838p2/5092518.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neal Shusterman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neal Shusterman" href="/user/show/5092518-neal-shusterman">Neal Shusterman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer232962579" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13807588033824507094">Thrilling, well told, and very, very dark. I listened to this book on Audio, and found I could not stop listening. I was still tuned in at the wee hours of the morning. I was surprised at how very dark and grotesque some of the descriptions were -- especially considering that this is a young adult novel, but the literary quality is so strong, it doesn’t detract from it. It felt like something Poe might write if he lived today.</span>

<div id="review_1743647513" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1743647513" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1743647513?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=halloween-bingo-2016">halloween-bingo-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1743647513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1112572785113233841">Actual rating: 3.5 stars.<br/><br/>A pseudo-Victorian novel set in 1888, <i>The Monstrumologist</i> has the same rather over-wrought style of that time period and is chock full of orphans, including our protagonist Will Henry. But this is very much a product of the twenty first century, being much more direct and much more graphic than the standard Victorian novel.<br/><br/>On full display is the mad scientist stereotype. The doctor whom Will Henry serves is depicted as amoral, pursuing scientific knowledge without much r</span>
  <span id="freeText1112572785113233841" style="display:none">Actual rating: 3.5 stars.<br /><br />A pseudo-Victorian novel set in 1888, <i>The Monstrumologist</i> has the same rather over-wrought style of that time period and is chock full of orphans, including our protagonist Will Henry. But this is very much a product of the twenty first century, being much more direct and much more graphic than the standard Victorian novel.<br /><br />On full display is the mad scientist stereotype. The doctor whom Will Henry serves is depicted as amoral, pursuing scientific knowledge without much reference to morals or emotions. He attempts to be the ultimate unbiased observer. There is some exploration of the danger of obsession , with references to Nietzsche (referencing his statement: If you gaze long into an abyss, the abyss also gazes into you). Indeed, by book’s end, the reader can certainly see where the doctor’s childhood has shaped the nature of the conflict, which is interesting considering that Sigmund Freud’s theories were developed during the Victorian period and are generally accepted into popular thinking today. <br /><br />The mad scientist stereotype always frustrates me, appearing as it does from Mary Shelley’s <i>Frankenstein</i> right through to Michael Crichton’s <i>Jurassic Park</i>. It makes a good story, which is why it continues to be used, but it also feeds that strain of scientific ignorance and fear that seems to run just beneath the surface of so many issues of our times. Both industrialists and environmentalists who refuse to believe various scientific studies, for example, and have dug into their positions. Rather than actually think and truly negotiate, they merely refuse to believe each’s other’s positions and go nowhere and nothing changes. (Science is a method of investigation, not a religious belief.)<br /><br />For those with delicate sensibilities, this may be a book to avoid as there is a lot of what I found to be gratuitous gore. But there are a few interesting ideas being bounced around and once again, I find myself impressed a work of YA fiction. <br /><br />Read to fill the <b>YA Horror</b> square of my <b>2016 Halloween Bingo</b> card.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1112572785113233841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_577314592" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/577314592" />
    <a title="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies"><img alt="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500999223p2/4527753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/577314592?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies" href="/user/show/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies">Khanh, first of her name, mother of bunnies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies?shelf=uk">uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies?shelf=why-do-i-hate-myself">why-do-i-hate-myself</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies?shelf=middle-school">middle-school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4527753-khanh-first-of-her-name-mother-of-bunnies?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer577314592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12307445936247205361">I'm not bothered by gore, I love it, and I think I've been so desensitized by blood/explosions/murder from exposure to movies and books from an early age that I am largely unaffected by it now. That said, I do not think a child would be immune to such horrors. Will Henry, the protagonist, is a 11/12 year old orphan, who has been brought in to be the slave to Dr. Warthrop. Now, you could call him Dr. Warthrop's ward, guardian, adopted father, but all that would be false. Dr. Warthrop is ice-cold,</span>
  <span id="freeText12307445936247205361" style="display:none">I'm not bothered by gore, I love it, and I think I've been so desensitized by blood/explosions/murder from exposure to movies and books from an early age that I am largely unaffected by it now. That said, I do not think a child would be immune to such horrors. Will Henry, the protagonist, is a 11/12 year old orphan, who has been brought in to be the slave to Dr. Warthrop. Now, you could call him Dr. Warthrop's ward, guardian, adopted father, but all that would be false. Dr. Warthrop is ice-cold, unfeeling, completely idiotic in his ability to raise a desert plant that needs no watering, much less a very young, impressionable boy.<br /><br />This book is simply unbelievable. An 11-year old boy is forced to wake up in the middle of the night to confront the dissection and destruction of a grafted horror of a monster that has been dug from the grave? He hasn't even been enslav--- I mean, apprenticed to Dr. Warthrop for that long, and he does it all with nary a word of protest? No, not realistic. I don't care about his background, I don't care about his brilliancy, Warthrop is an annoying, sadistic son of a bitch, and I could not bear him for the entire book. Will Henry, as mature as he comes across---as he has to be, comes off as spineless and weak. He barely has a voice in the book, and I can't imagine his voice in my head speaking louder than a whisper over Warthrop's whining, nasal screech. Every character has their own narrative voice in my head, and those were my interpretation of theirs.<br /><br />I am reminded of another series for a younger audience, also in the horror genre, <i>The Spook's Apprentice</i>. For the first few books at least, there is no comparison to this one. Tom Ward is also apprenticed at a young age to a much older, experienced man, but I actually think the Spook has kindness and character, Tom has more of a backbone, and the horror is actually more realistic, given the genre. Read that instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12307445936247205361" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
